He's the founder of Small AI. So very roughly speaking, capability means what a model could do at certain points of time. For those of you who are technically minded, this means the pass at k accuracy of a model for a very high k. That means that one of the k answers that the model outputs are correct.

On the other hand, reliability means consistently getting the answer right each and every single time. When agents are deployed for consequential decisions in the real world, what you really need to focus on is reliability rather than capability. That's because language models are already capable of very many things. But if you trick yourself into believing this means a reliable experience for the end user, that's when products in the real world go wrong.

So in particular, I think the methods for training models that get us to the 90 percent of it, what in Swift's term would be the job of a machine learning engineer, don't necessarily get us to the 99.999 percent, what is often known as the five nines of reliability. Closing this gap between the 90 percent and the 99.9 percent is the job of an AI engineer.

And I think this is what has led to the failures of products like Humane Spin and Rabbit R1. One, it's because the developers did not anticipate that not having reliability in products like these would lead to a product failure. In other words, if your personal assistant only offers your orders, your Doe Dash food correctly 80 percent of the times, that is a catastrophic failure from the point of view of a product.

Now, one thing people have proposed to fix this sort of issue, to improve reliability, is to create a verifier. Something like a unit test. And on this basis, there have been several claims that if we could improve the inference scaling capabilities of these tools and get to very reliable models.

algorithms. Unfortunately, what we've found is that verifiers can also be imperfect in practice. For instance, two of the leading coding benchmarks, Humane Val and MBPP, both have false positives in their unit tests. That is, a model could output incorrect code and still pass the unit test. And once we account for these false positives, what we have are these inference scaling curves bending downwards.

So rather than model performance continuing to improve, if there are false positives in your verifiers, the model performance sort of bends downwards, simply because the more you try, the more likely it is you'll get a wrong answer. And so this is also not a perfect solution to the problem of reliability.

So what is the solution? I think the challenge for AI engineers is to figure out what sorts of software optimizations and abstractions are needed for working with inherently stochastic components like LLMs. In other words, it's a system design problem rather than just a modeling problem where you need to work around the constraints of an inherently stochastic system.

And I want to argue in the last one minute of my talk that this means looking at AI engineering as more of a reliability engineering field than a software or machine learning engineering field. And this also brings me to the clear mindset shift that is needed to become successful from the perspective of being an AI engineer.

If you look at the title slide of my talk, this title slide sort of pointed to one such area where we've already overcome certain types of limitations of stochastic systems. And that is with the birth of computing. The 1946 ENIAC computer used over 17,000 vacuum tubes, many of which at the beginning of this process, used to fail so often that the computer was unavailable half the time.

And the engineers who built this product knew that this is a failure from the point of view of the end users. So their primary job in the first two years of this computer was to fix the reliability issues. To reduce it to a point where it becomes well enough, it works well enough to become usable by the end user.

And I say that this is precisely what AI engineers need to be thinking about as their real job. It is not to create excellent products, though that is important, but rather to fix the reliability issues that plague every single agent that uses inherently stochastic models as its basis. So this is what I leave you here with today.

To become successful engineers, you need a reliability shift in your mindset to think of yourselves as the people who are ensuring that this next wave of computing is as reliable for end users as possible. And there's a lot of precedent for this type of thing happening in the past.

All right. With this, I'll leave you with the key takeaways. It was a pleasure being here. Thank you. Let's dive with our next presenters into Gemini Deep Research. Arush Selvan: But you've got to do it in five minutes, because beyond that, we don't have the chips.

Arush Selvan: And so we needed to figure out how do you sort of build asynchronous experiences in an inherently synchronous product. Arush Selvan: You also wanted to set expectations with users.

Arush Selvan: Deep research is good for one very specific thing, but a lot of user queries to Gemini are things like, "What's the weather? Arush Selvan: Write me a joke." Arush Selvan: Things like that, where waiting five minutes is not going to get you a good answer, Arush Selvan: and we wanted to set expectations.

Arush Selvan: And the last thing is, our answers can be thousands of words long, and we needed to figure out how do you make it easy for users to engage with really long outputs in a chat experience. Arush Selvan: So let's walk through kind of the UX and kind of think about how we solve some of these.

Arush Selvan: So imagine you're a VC, and everybody's talking about investing in nuclear in America, Arush Selvan: So you come with this query like, "Hey, help me learn the latest technology breakthroughs in small nuclear reactors, and tell me interesting companies in the supply chain." Arush Selvan: So the first step when you bring this query to deep research is that Gemini will actually put together a research plan for you and present it in a card.

Arush Selvan: And so this is the first way in which we're able to communicate with users. Arush Selvan: This is different. Arush Selvan: This isn't your standard chatbot experience. Arush Selvan: Something's going to happen. Arush Selvan: You're going to hit start. Arush Selvan: But it's also an opportunity for us to actually show the user a research plan that they can edit and engage with.

Arush Selvan: Kind of like a good analyst. Arush Selvan: Right? Arush Selvan: They wouldn't just get to work. Arush Selvan: They'd actually show you, "Okay, here's how I'm going to approach this." Arush Selvan: And it's a way for users to, if they want, kind of engage and steer the direction of the research further.

Arush Selvan: Now, once you hit start, we actually try and show you what Gemini is doing under the hood in real time by showing you the websites it's browsing. Arush Selvan: And this is a feature that was built before Thinking Models, and thoughts are also a really great way of kind of showing transparency of what the model is thinking.

Arush Selvan: But what's really nice here is while you wait, you can sort of click through the websites, dive into any of the content, but what we also inadvertently saw is people trying to game that number to see how high it could go. Arush Selvan: And so we definitely saw people push that number into the thousands to try and see how many websites Deep Research could read.

Arush Selvan: Finally, we kind of get this report that's thousands of ways long. And we're really inspired by what kind of what Anthropic does with artifacts, and so we thought that was a really great way of sort of being able to pin an artifact so that users can actually ask questions about the research while reading the material.

Arush Selvan: They don't have to scroll back and forth, and what's really neat about this is it means it's easy for you to engage in sort of changing the style of the report, adding sections, removing sections, asking follow up questions, and it sort of makes that really easy. Arush Selvan: And the last part that's super important is kind of user trust, and also doing right by the publishers.

So we try and always show as all the sources we read, as well as all the sources we used in the report, because not everything that we read is used, but it stays in context for follow up questions. Arush Selvan: And also sort of these are all things that carry over to Google Docs, citations, and things like that, if you choose to export.

Arush Selvan: So I thought today we can pick some of the challenges that one has to encounter while building a research agent, and talk through some of them. Arush Selvan: So I picked four for today. Arush Selvan: So one is this long-running nature of tasks introduce a couple of things that we need to look into.

Arush Selvan: Second is the model has to plan iteratively and spend its time and compute during this time effectively. Arush Selvan: So what are those challenges there? Arush Selvan: And it has to do this while interacting with a very noisy environment that is the web. Arush Selvan: And as you do this and read through information, very quickly you can start seeing your context grow, and how do you effectively manage context.

Arush Selvan: So if you think about a job that runs for multiple minutes, and something that can make many, many different LLM calls and calls to different services, they're bound to be failures. Arush Selvan: Right? Arush Selvan: And today we are talking about minutes, but you can very easily think in the future of these kind of research agents taking like multiple hours.

Arush Selvan: So it's important to be robust to intermediate failures of these various services of various liabilities. Arush Selvan: And so being able to build a good state management solution, being able to recover Arush Selvan: from errors effectively so that you just don't drop the whole research task due to one failure.

Arush Selvan: That's one. Arush Selvan: The second aspect of doing this, what it enables us is to enable this feature Arush Selvan: cross-platform, so we believe more and more users will start kind of registering your asks, or your research tasks, and just like walk away, do their thing, and then you need to get notified.

Arush Selvan: And this can happen now across devices, and you can pick off reading it once it's done. Arush Selvan: So now what is the model doing through these few minutes? Arush Selvan: So let's take an example, right? Arush Selvan: So here we're looking for athletic scholarships for short-put.

Arush Selvan: There are many facets to this query, and we kind of show this in a research plan like Arush showed. Arush Selvan: The first thing the model has to do is try to figure out which of these sub-problems it can start tackling in parallel versus things that are inherently sequential, right?

Arush Selvan: So the model has to be able to reason to do that. Arush Selvan: And the other challenge is, here you see you're always going to land in this state where there's partial information, so it's important to look at all the information found so far before you decide what to do next.

Arush Selvan: So in this instance, the model found, hey, it knows the qualifying standards for the D1 division, but in order to provide a complete report and answer the user's question, Arush Selvan: It has to go figure out what the equivalent for the D2 and D3 divisions are. Arush Selvan: So this notion of being able to ground on information you find and then plan your next step is key.

Arush Selvan: Another example of partial information could be when you make searches. Arush Selvan: So in this case, you're trying to find the best roller coaster for kids. Arush Selvan: You might find results that provide partial information again. Arush Selvan: So here you end up at a link which talks about the top ten roller coasters, but does not mention anything about them being suitable to kids.

Arush Selvan: So the plan has to recognize this fact and then go ahead and in the next steps Arush Selvan: of planning try to dissolve this disambiguity. Arush Selvan: Another example of challenges in planning is information is often not found in one place. Arush Selvan: You find facets of information spread across different sources.

Arush Selvan: So here you're trying to find what would what would it take to get a certification for a scuba dive in some dive centers nearby. Arush Selvan: So you see one part or one source has the kind of the structure of what what you have to go through to get a certification, but in a completely different source you have this notion of the pricing for this diving center.

Arush Selvan: So the model has to weave this together to figure out what the cost structure Arush Selvan: for such a certification would look like. Arush Selvan: Then there's the classic entity resolution problem. Arush Selvan: So you might find mentions of the same entity across different sources. Arush Selvan: So you need to be able to reason about some information indicators to kind of figure out if they're talking about the same entity or you need to explore more to verify such disambiguities.

Arush Selvan: Yeah, I think most people here have worked on some notion of a web problem. Arush Selvan: And we know like it's super fragmented. Arush Selvan: So here you see two different websites talking about the same thing about music festivals Arush Selvan: in Portugal this year on the left.

Arush Selvan: On the left, if you end up at such a website, it's easier and you get most of your information in one go. Arush Selvan: On the right, the layout is different, so having a robust browsing mechanism if you want Arush Selvan: To navigate the web for your research tasks is another important challenge.

Arush Selvan: So like we saw, there is a lot of these intermediate outputs, and as you do this Arush Selvan: and you start getting streams of information during your planning. You can imagine your context size growing very quickly. Arush Selvan: The other challenge that about context size is your research task doesn't typically end with your first query.

Arush Selvan: People have follow ups. People can say, hey, can you also do the same for this other topic? Arush Selvan: So there is like this kind of a follow up, a deep research, and that also adds pressure on the context. Arush Selvan: We at Gemini have the liberty of really long context models, but even then you have to design Arush Selvan: Some way to make sure you effectively manage your context.

And there are multiple choices here. Each come with various different trade-offs. Arush Selvan: We're showing one here where we kind of have like this recency bias. So you have a lot more information about your current and your previous tasks. Arush Selvan: But as you get to older tasks, we kind of selectively pick out, you know, things what we call as research notes and put it in a rag.

Arush Selvan: That way, the model can still access it, but it's being selective. Arush Selvan: I'll hand it back to Arush to talk about what's next. Arush Selvan: Yeah, so we were super excited to put this feature out in December. We weren't actually sure if anyone was going to use it, if anyone was going to care to wait five minutes for something, and we were really positively surprised by the reception.

Arush Selvan: And really what we saw was, hey, we've built something that's maybe as good as like a McKinsey analyst, right? And we give it away for 20 bucks. But, you know, that's really great. Arush Selvan: And, but what it does is it just retrieves from the open web, and it's a text in, text out only system, right?

And so where we sort of, we sort of see a few different directions of where research agents are going to go next. Arush Selvan: And the first one is around expertise, right? So how do you go from a McKinsey analyst to a McKinsey partner, or a Goldman Sachs partner, or like a partner at a law firm, right?

So that's really around not just being able to aggregate information and synthesize it, but also think through the so what of how do, like, what are the implications for what we're going to do, and what are the most interesting insights and patterns that come out of it? Arush Selvan: The, the other thing is, you know, there are plenty of domains beyond professional services, like the sciences, where you, you know, want to get really good, you know, you want something that can read many papers, form hypotheses, find really interesting patterns in, you know, what methods we used, and, and come up with novel hypotheses to explore.

Arush Selvan: However, just because you build something that can be really smart, doesn't mean that it's useful to someone, right? So, if we were thinking about a use case of running a due diligence Arush Selvan: on a company, the way you'd present that information to me, would be very different to the way you'd present that information to say a Goldman Sachs banker, right?

Arush Selvan: For me, you really want to talk through, like, what, like, what is this company, and how's its position strategically, but a banker would want to know all the financial information, actually have a DCF that they could look at, right? Arush Selvan: Actually have a, have a much more, like, fine-grained, sort of, finance, financial modeling and analysis, and, and that really should shape the way in which you browse the web, right?

Arush Selvan: The way you browse the web, the way you frame your answer, the kind of questions you pursue, should be very personalized to kind of meeting the user where they're at. Arush Selvan: I think the last part is sort of something that goes across domains of what models can do, right?

Arush Selvan: So not just being able to do web research with text, but being able to combine that with abilities in coding, data science, even video generation, right? Arush Selvan: So coming back to this example, if you're doing a due diligence, what if it could go and do, like, a lot of statistical analysis and actually build financial models to inform the research output that it gives you, right?

Arush Selvan: Telling you, hey, why is this a good company or not? Arush Selvan: I should say Google doesn't give financial advice, and, you know, it's not a financial advisor. Arush Selvan: But yeah, and so we're really excited about the potential. Arush Selvan: We think there's a ton of headroom to make research agents better.

Arush Selvan: And we are really glad we didn't call this Gemini Deep Dive, which was our best name before, before launching this feature. Arush Selvan: That's it. Arush Selvan: Thank you so much. Arush Selvan: Thank you. Arush Selvan: Our next presenter is member of technical staff at Anthropic, here to present how they build effective agents.

Arush Selvan: Please join me in welcoming to the stage Barry Zhang. Arush Selvan: All right, you guys hear me? Arush Selvan: Yeah. Arush Selvan: All right, awesome. Arush Selvan: Wow, it's incredible to be on the same stage as so many people I've learned so much from. Arush Selvan: Let's get into it.

Arush Selvan: My name is Barry, and today we're going to be talking about how we build effective agents. Arush Selvan: About two months ago, Eric and I wrote a blog post called Building Effective Agents. Arush Selvan: In there, we shared some opinionated take on what an agent is and isn't, and we give some practical learnings that we have gained along the way.

Arush Selvan: Today, I'd like to go deeper on three core ideas from the blog post and provide you with some personal musings at the end. Arush Selvan: Here are those ideas. Arush Selvan: First, don't build agents for everything. Arush Selvan: Second, keep it simple. Arush Selvan: And third, think like your agents.

Arush Selvan: Let's first start with a recap of how we got here. Arush Selvan: Most of us probably started building very simple features, things like summarization, classification, extraction, just really simple things that felt like magic two to three years ago and have now become table stakes. Arush Selvan: Then as we got more sophisticated and as products mature, we got more creative.

Arush Selvan: One model call often wasn't enough. Arush Selvan: So we started orchestrating multiple model calls in predefined control flows. Arush Selvan: This basically gave us a way to trade off cost and latency for better performance. Arush Selvan: And we call these workflows. Arush Selvan: We believe this is the beginning of agentic systems.

Arush Selvan: Now models are even more capable and we're seeing more and more domain specific agents start to pop up in production. Arush Selvan: Unlike workflows, agents can decide their own trajectory and operate almost independently based on environment feedback. Arush Selvan: This is going to be our focus today.

Arush Selvan: It's probably a little bit too early to name what the next phase of agentic system is going to look like, especially in production. Arush Selvan: Single agents could become a lot more general purpose and more capable, or we can start to see collaboration and delegation in multi-agent settings.

Arush Selvan: Regardless, I think the broad trend here is that as we give these systems a lot more agency, they become more useful and more capable. Arush Selvan: But as a result, the cost, the latency, the consequences of errors also go up. Arush Selvan: And that brings us to the first point: Don't build agents for everything.

Arush Selvan: Well, why not? Arush Selvan: We think of agents as a way to scale complex and valuable tasks. Arush Selvan: They shouldn't be a drop-in upgrade for every use case. Arush Selvan: If you haven't read the blog post, you'll know that we talked a lot about workflows. Arush Selvan: And that's because we really like them, and they're a great, concrete way to deliver values today.

Arush Selvan: Well, so when should you build an agent? Arush Selvan: Here's our checklist. Arush Selvan: The first thing to consider is the complexity of your task. Arush Selvan: Agents really thrive in ambiguous problem spaces, and if you can map out the entire decision tree pretty easily, just build that explicitly and then optimize every node of that decision tree.

Arush Selvan: It's a lot more cost-effective, and it's going to give you a lot more control. Arush Selvan: Next thing to consider is the value of your task. Arush Selvan: The exploration I just mentioned is going to cost you a lot of tokens. Arush Selvan: So the task really needs to justify the cost.

Arush Selvan: If your budget per task is around 10 cents, for example, you're building a high-volume customer support system, Arush Selvan: that only affords you 30 to 50 thousand tokens. Arush Selvan: In that case, just use a workflow to solve the most common scenarios, and you're able to capture the majority of the values from there.

Arush Selvan: On the other hand, though, if you look at this question and your first thought is, Arush Selvan: I don't care how many tokens I spend, I just want to get the task done. Arush Selvan: Please see me after the talk. Arush Selvan: Our go-to-market team would love to speak with you.

Arush Selvan: From there, we want to de-risk the critical capabilities. Arush Selvan: This is to make sure that there aren't any significant bottlenecks in the agent's trajectory. Arush Selvan: If you're doing a coding agent, you want to make sure it's able to write good code, it's able to debug, and it's able to recover from its errors.

Arush Selvan: If you do have bottlenecks, that's probably not going to be fatal, but they will multiply your cost and latency. Arush Selvan: So in that case, we normally just reduce the scope, simplify the task, and try again. Arush Selvan: Finally, the last important thing to consider is the cost of error and error discovery.

Arush Selvan: If your errors are going to be high stake and very hard to discover, it's going to be very difficult for you to trust the agent to take actions on your behalf and to have more autonomy. Arush Selvan: You can always mitigate this by limiting the scope, right?

Arush Selvan: You can have read-only access, you can have more human in the loop, but this will also limit how well you're able to scale your agent in your use case. Arush Selvan: Let's see this checklist in action. Arush Selvan: Why is coding a great agent use case? Arush Selvan: First, to go from design doc to a PR is obviously a very ambiguous and very complex task.

Arush Selvan: And second, a lot of us are developers here, so we know that good code has a lot of value. Arush Selvan: And third, many of us already use cloud for coding, so we know that it's great at many parts of the coding workflow. Arush Selvan: And last, coding has this really nice property where the output is easily verifiable through unit test and CI.

Arush Selvan: And that's probably why we're seeing so many creative and successful coding agents right now. Arush Selvan: Once you find a good use case for agents, this is the second core idea, which is to keep it as simple as possible. Arush Selvan: Let me show you what I mean.

Arush Selvan: This is what agents look like to us. Arush Selvan: They're models using tools in a loop. Arush Selvan: In this frame, three components define what an agent really looks like. Arush Selvan: First is the environment. Arush Selvan: This is the system that the agent is operating in.

Arush Selvan: Then we have a set of tools, which offer an interface for the agent to take action and get feedback. Arush Selvan: Then we have the system prompt, which defines the goals, the constraints, and the ideal behavior for the agent to actually work in this environment. Arush Selvan: Then the model gets called in a loop, and that's agents.

Arush Selvan: We have learned the hard way to keep this simple because any complexity upfront is really going to kill iteration speed. Arush Selvan: Iterating on just these three basic components is going to give you by far the highest ROI, and optimizations can come later. Arush Selvan: Here are examples of three agent use cases that we have built for ourselves or our customers, just to make it more concrete.

Arush Selvan: They're going to look very different on the product surface. Arush Selvan: They're going to look very different in their scope. Arush Selvan: They're going to look different in their capability. Arush Selvan: But they share almost exactly the same backbone. Arush Selvan: They actually share almost the exact same code.

Arush Selvan: The environment largely depends on your use case. Arush Selvan: So really, the only two design decisions is what are the set of tools you want to offer to the agent, and what is the prompt that you want to instruct your agent to follow? Arush Selvan: On this note, if you want to learn more about tools, my friend Mahesh is going to be giving a workshop on model context protocol, MCP, tomorrow morning.

Arush Selvan: I've seen that workshop. It's going to be really fun, so I highly encourage you guys to check that out. Arush Selvan: But back to our talk. Arush Selvan: Once you have figured out these three basic components, you have a lot of optimization to do from there. Arush Selvan: For coding and computer use, you might want to catch the trajectory to reduce cost.

Arush Selvan: For search, where you have a lot of tool calls, you can parallelize a lot of those to reduce latency. Arush Selvan: And for almost all of these, we want to make sure to present the agent's progress in such a way that gain user trust. Arush Selvan: But that's it.

Keep it as simple as possible as you're iterating, build these three components first, and then optimize once you have the behaviors down. Arush Selvan: All right. This is the last idea. Arush Selvan: It's to think like your agents. Arush Selvan: I've seen a lot of builders, and myself included, who develop agents from our own perspectives and get confused when agents make a mistake.

Arush Selvan: It seems counterintuitive to us. Arush Selvan: And that's why we always recommend to put yourself in the agent's context window. Arush Selvan: Agents can exhibit some really sophisticated behavior. It can look incredibly complex. Arush Selvan: But at each step, what the model is doing is still just running inference on a very limited set of contexts.

Arush Selvan: Everything that the model knows about the current state of the world is going to be explained in that 10 to 20k tokens. Arush Selvan: And it's really helpful to limit ourselves in that context and see if it's actually sufficient and coherent. Arush Selvan: This will give you a much better understanding of how agents see the world and then kind of bridge the gap between our understanding and theirs.

Arush Selvan: Let's imagine for a second that we're computer use agents now and then see what that feels like. Arush Selvan: All we're going to get is a static screenshot and a very poorly written description. Arush Selvan: This is about yours truly. Let's read through it. You know, you're a computer use agent.

Arush Selvan: You have a set of tools and you have a task. Terrible. Arush Selvan: We can think and talk and reason what we want, but the only thing that's going to take effect in the environment are our tools. Arush Selvan: So we attempt a click without really seeing what's happening and while the inference is happening, while the tool execution is happening, Arush Selvan: This is basically equivalent to us closing our eyes for three to five seconds and using the computer in the dark.

Arush Selvan: Then you open up your eyes and you see another screenshot. Whatever you did could have worked or you could have shut down the computer. Arush Selvan: You just don't know. This is a huge lethal phase and the cycle kind of starts again. Arush Selvan: I highly recommend just trying - try doing a full task from the agent's perspective like this.

Arush Selvan: I promise you it's a fascinating and only mildly uncomfortable experience. Arush Selvan: However, once you go through that mildly uncomfortable experience, I think it becomes very clear what the agents would have actually needed. Arush Selvan: It's clearly very crucial to know what the screen resolution is so I know how to click.

Arush Selvan: It's also good to have recommended actions and limitations just so that, you know, we can put some guardrails around what we should be exploring and we can avoid unnecessary exploration. Arush Selvan: These are just some examples and, you know, do this exercise for your own agent use case and figure out what kind of context do you actually want to provide for the agent.

Arush Selvan: Fortunately, though, we are building systems that speak our language. So we could just ask cloud to understand cloud. Arush Selvan: You can throw in your system prompt and ask, well, is any of this instruction ambiguous? Does it make sense to you? Are you able to follow this?

Arush Selvan: You can throw in your true description and see whether the agent knows how to use the tool. You can see if it wants more parameter, fewer parameter. Arush Selvan: And one thing that we do quite frequently is we throw the entire agent's trajectory into cloud and just ask it, hey, why do you think we made this decision right here?

Arush Selvan: And is there anything that we can do to help you make better decisions? Arush Selvan: This shouldn't replace your own understanding of the context, but it will help you gain a much closer perspective on how the agent is seeing the world. Arush Selvan: So once again, think like your agent as you're iterating.

Arush Selvan: All right, I've spent most of the talk talking about very practical stuff. I'm going to indulge myself and spend one slide on personal musings. Arush Selvan: This is going to be my view on how this might evolve and some open questions I think we need to answer together as AI engineers.

Arush Selvan: These are the top three things that are always on my mind. First, I think we need to make agents a lot more budget aware. Arush Selvan: Unlike workflows, we don't really have a great sense of control for the cost and latency for agents. I think figuring this out will enable a lot more use cases as it gives us a necessary control to deploy them in production.

Arush Selvan: The open question is just what's the best way to define and enforce budgets in terms of time, in terms of money, in terms of tokens, the things that we care about. Arush Selvan: Next up is this concept of self-evolving tools. I've already hinted at this two slides ago, but we are already using models to help iterate on the tool description.

Arush Selvan: But this should generalize pretty well into a meta tool where agents can design and improve their own tool ergonomics. Arush Selvan: This will make agents a lot more general purpose as they can adopt the tools that they need for each use case. Arush Selvan: Finally, I don't even think this is a hot take anymore.

I have a personal conviction that we will see a lot more multi-agent collaborations in production by the end of this year. Arush Selvan: They're well-parallelized, they have very nice separation of concerns, and having sub-agent, for example, will really protect the main agent's context window. Arush Selvan: But I think a big open question here is how do these agents actually communicate with each other?

Arush Selvan: We're currently in this very rigid frame of having mostly synchronous user assistant turns, and I think most of our systems are built around that. Arush Selvan: So how do we expand from there and build an asynchronous communication, and enable more roles that afford agents to communicate with each other and recognize each other?

Arush Selvan: I think that's going to be a big open question as we explore this more multi-agent future. Arush Selvan: These are the areas that take up a lot of my mind space. If you're also thinking about this, please shoot me a text. I would love to chat. Arush Selvan: Okay, let's bring it all together.

If you forget everything I said today, these are the three takeaways. Arush Selvan: First, don't build agents for everything. If you do find a good use case and want to build an agent, keep it as simple for as long as possible. Arush Selvan: And finally, as you iterate, try to think like your agent, gain their perspective, and help them do their job.

Arush Selvan: I would love to keep in touch with every one of you. If you want to chat about agents, especially those open questions that I talked about, you'll be incredibly lovely. Arush Selvan: And so thank you for your time today. Arush Selvan: I look forward to a lot of engaging discussions, and it's great to talk to you all. Arush Selvan: Our next presenter is a researcher at Morgan Stanley. Arush Selvan: Please join me in welcoming to the stage, Will Brown.

Arush Selvan: Hello, everyone. Arush Selvan: Thanks, Rick and the whole AI engineer conference team for putting this together and having me. Arush Selvan: I am Will Brown. Arush Selvan: I am a machine learning researcher at Morgan Stanley. Arush Selvan: And today I want to talk to you all a bit about what I think reinforcement learning, or RL, means for agents.

Arush Selvan: So I was in grad school at Columbia for a while and I mostly worked on theory for multi-agent reinforcement learning. Arush Selvan: And over the past couple of years I have been working at Morgan Stanley on a wide range of LLM related projects, some of which look kind of like agents, but I will not really be talking too much about that today.

Arush Selvan: I'm also relatively active on X, the everything app, and that will become relevant later in the talk. Arush Selvan: This talk I think will be probably a little different from most of the talks at the conference. Arush Selvan: It's not about things we ship to prod. Arush Selvan: It's not about things that definitely work and you should go do tomorrow that are like proven science or best practices.

Arush Selvan: It's about where we might be headed and I want to really just tell a story that will synthesize some things that have been happening in the broader research community. Arush Selvan: And where these trends might be pointing, do some speculation, and also talk about some recent open source work of my own.

Arush Selvan: And the goal of this is to help you plan and understand what reinforcement learning means, what it means for agents, and how to best be ready for a potential future which may involve reinforcement learning as part of the agent engineering loop. Arush Selvan: So, where are we today?

Arush Selvan: Most LLMs that we work with are essentially chatbots. Arush Selvan: I think it's helpful to think about OpenAI's five levels framework here. Arush Selvan: So, we did pretty well with chatbots. Arush Selvan: Seems like we're doing pretty well with reasoners. Arush Selvan: These are great models for question and answer.

Arush Selvan: They're very helpful for interactive problem solving. Arush Selvan: We have the 01, 03, R1, Grok3, Gemini, et cetera, models that are really good at kind of thinking longer. Arush Selvan: And we're trying to figure out how we take all of this and make agents level three. Arush Selvan: And these are systems that are taking actions.

Arush Selvan: These are systems that are doing things that are longer and harder and more complex. Arush Selvan: And currently, the way we tend to do this is chaining together multiple calls to these underlying chatbot or reasoner LLMs. Arush Selvan: And we do lots of things like prompt engineering, tool calling, evals, ops, giving the models tools of their own to use, having humans in the loop.

Arush Selvan: And the results are like pretty good. Arush Selvan: There's a lot of things that we can do. Arush Selvan: And then there's a lot of stuff that it feels like is around the corner that we're all imagining about AGI. Arush Selvan: But we're not really to the point yet where these things are going off and doing the things that we would imagine an AGI is really doing to the degree of autonomy that that would, I presume, entail.

Arush Selvan: So I think it's useful a bit to distinguish between agents and pipelines. Arush Selvan: I think Barry's talk earlier was a good way to kind of frame this. Arush Selvan: I'm going to use pipelines to encapsulate what Barry called workflows. Arush Selvan: And I think these are really systems with fairly low degrees of autonomy.

Arush Selvan: And there's a very nontrivial amount of engineering required to determine these decision trees to say, how does one action or call flow into another? Arush Selvan: How do we find the prompts? Arush Selvan: And it seems like a lot of the winning apps in the agent space have very tight feedback loops.

Arush Selvan: And so whether or not you want to call these agents or pipelines, these are things where a user is interacting with some sort of interface. Arush Selvan: They're telling it what to do. Arush Selvan: The thing will do some stuff and come back relatively quickly. Arush Selvan: Things like the IDEs like Cursor, Windsurf, and Replit.

Arush Selvan: And search tools that are really good at harder question answer, maybe with some web search or research integrated. Arush Selvan: But there's not that many agents nowadays that will go off and do stuff for more than 10 minutes at a time. Arush Selvan: I think Devon, Operator, and OpenAI's deep research are the three that really come to mind as like feeling a little more in the like autonomous agent direction.

Arush Selvan: And I think a lot of us might be wondering, how do we make more of these? Arush Selvan: And the kind of traditional wisdom is like, okay, we'll just wait for better models. Arush Selvan: Once better models are around, we can just like use those, we'll be good.

Arush Selvan: But I think it's also to kind of take note of like the traditional definition of reinforcement learning. Arush Selvan: And what an agent means there, which is this idea of a thing that is interacting with an environment with a goal. Arush Selvan: And the goal that, and the system is designed to learn how to get better at that goal over time via repeated interaction with the system.

Arush Selvan: And I think this is something that a lot of us are either doing manually or don't really have the tools to do, Arush Selvan: Which is once we have our thing, that it's set up to make the calls we want, and the performance is like 70%. Arush Selvan: And we've done a lot of prompt tuning and we wanted to get it up to 90%.

Arush Selvan: But we just like don't have the models to do it or the models struggle to get the success. Arush Selvan: What's our path forward? Arush Selvan: And so in terms of model trends, I think I won't spend too much time talking about this. Arush Selvan: But pre-training seems to be having diminishing returns to capital at least.

Arush Selvan: We're still seeing kind of like loss go down, but it does kind of feel like we need new tricks. Arush Selvan: Reinforcement learning from human feedback is great for making kind of friendly chatbots. Arush Selvan: But it doesn't really seem to be continually pushing us at the frontier of smarter and smarter and smarter models.

Arush Selvan: We talk a lot about synthetic data and I think synthetic data is great for distilling larger models down into smaller models to have kind of really tiny models that are really performant. Arush Selvan: But on its own, it doesn't seem to be an unlock for like massive capabilities getting better and better.

Arush Selvan: Unless we throw in verification in the loop or rejection sampling or any of these things. Arush Selvan: And that really takes us to the world of reinforcement learning where this seems to be the trick that unlocked test time scaling for 01 models in R1. Arush Selvan: It's not bottlenecked by needing manually curated human data and it does seem to actually work.

Arush Selvan: I think we all kind of took note about a month ago when DeepSeq released the R1 model and paper to the world. Arush Selvan: And I think this was really exciting because it was the first paper that really explained how you build a thing like 01. Arush Selvan: We had kind of speculation and some rumors, but they really laid out the algorithm and the mechanisms for what it takes to get a model to learn to do this kind of reasoning.

Arush Selvan: And it turns out it was essentially just reinforcement learning where you give the model some questions, you measure if it's getting the answer right, and you just kind of turn this crank of giving it feedback to do more like the things that worked well and less like the things that didn't work.

Arush Selvan: And what you end up seeing is that the long chain of thought from models like 01 and R1 actually emerges as a byproduct of this. Arush Selvan: It wasn't kind of manually programmed in where the models were like given data of like 10,000 token reasoning steps. Arush Selvan: This was the thing the model learned to do because it was a good strategy.

Arush Selvan: And reinforcement learning at the core is really about identifying good strategies for solving problems. Arush Selvan: It also seems like open source models are back in a big way. There's a lot of excitement around the open source community. Arush Selvan: People have been working on replication efforts for the 01 project and have also been trying to distill data from 01 down to smaller models.

Arush Selvan: And so what next? How does this relate to agents? Arush Selvan: I think it'll be helpful to know a little bit about how reinforcement learning works. Arush Selvan: The key idea is to explore and exploit. So you want to try stuff, see what works, do more of the things that worked, less of the things that didn't.

Arush Selvan: And so in this feedback loop demonstrated here in the image, we can see a challenge where a model is supposed to be writing code to pass test cases Arush Selvan: And we give it rewards that correspond to things like formatting, using the right language, and then ultimately whether or not the test cases are passing.

Arush Selvan: And so this is kind of a numerical signal that rather than like training on data where we were kind of curating this in advance, Arush Selvan: We are letting the model do synthetic data rollouts and seeing scores from these rollouts, which then are fed back into the model.

Arush Selvan: And so the GRPO algorithm, which maybe some of you have heard about, is the algorithm DeepSeq used. Arush Selvan: I think it's less of like a technical breakthrough in terms of it being a really important new algorithm to study, but I think it's very conceptually simple. Arush Selvan: And I think it's a nice way to think about what reinforcement learning means.

Arush Selvan: And the idea really is just that you, for a given prompt, sample n completions, you score them all, and you tell the model be more like the ones with higher scores. Arush Selvan: This is still in kind of the single-turn reasoner model non-agentic world. Arush Selvan: And so the challenges that lie ahead are going to be about how do we take these ideas and extend them into more powerful, more agentic, more autonomous systems.

Arush Selvan: But we do know that it can be done. Arush Selvan: So OpenAI's deep research still has a lot of questions that we do not know the answers to about how it works, but they have told us that it was end-to-end reinforcement learning. Arush Selvan: And so this is a case where the model is taking up to potentially 100 different tool calls of browsing or querying different parts of the internet to synthesize a large answer.

Arush Selvan: And it does seem, I think, to many people's vibe check opinions, very impressive. Arush Selvan: But it also is not AGI in the sense of you can't get it to go work in a repo or solve hard software engineering tasks. Arush Selvan: And people have kind of anecdotally found that it does struggle a bit for out-of-distribution tasks.

Arush Selvan: If you want it to fill out a table with 100 very manual calculations, it can struggle there. Arush Selvan: And so it seems like reinforcement learning, on one hand, is a big unlock for new skills and more autonomy. Arush Selvan: But it's not a thing that, so far, has granted us agents that can just do everything and know how to solve all kinds of problems.

Arush Selvan: But it is a path forward for teaching a model skills and having the model learn how to get better at certain skills, particularly in conjunction with environments and tools and verification. Arush Selvan: And so there is infrastructure out there for doing this on our own, kind of.

Arush Selvan: A lot of it is still RLHF style, by which I mean it's about kind of single-turn interactions, where the goal is we have reward signals that come from kind of human data that has been combined into a reward model. Arush Selvan: And if we want to have RL agents becoming part of our systems, maybe we will get really good API services from the large labs that let us build these things and hook into GPT, whatever, or Claude, whatever, and train these sorts of models on our own with fine-tuning.

Arush Selvan: But we also don't really have these options yet. Arush Selvan: OpenAI has kind of teased their reinforcement fine-tuning feedback, but it's not multi-step tool calling yet. Arush Selvan: And so I think if we want to plan ahead, it's worth kind of noting and asking, what would this ecosystem look like?

Arush Selvan: And there's a lot of unknown questions like how much this will cost? Arush Selvan: How small can the models be? Arush Selvan: Will it generalize across tasks? Arush Selvan: And how do we design good rewards in good environments? Arush Selvan: And there's a lot of opportunity here.

Arush Selvan: Open source infrastructure, there's a lot of room to build and grow and determine what the best practices are going to be, what the right tools will be, Arush Selvan: As well as companies that can build tools to support this ecosystem, whether or not they're already in the fine-tuning world or not.

Arush Selvan: And services for supporting this kind of agentic RL. Arush Selvan: And I think also it is worth thinking about things that are like not literal RL in the sense of training the model, but at the prompt level, there's all sorts of automation we can do. Arush Selvan: So if you've used DSPy, I think that is kind of adjacent to RL in the flavor of having a signal that we can then bootstrap from to improve our underlying system based on improving some downstream scores.

Arush Selvan: Now I want to share a story with you about a single Python file I wrote a couple weeks ago. Arush Selvan: So this was the weekend after R1 came out, and I had been reading the paper and thought it was really cool. Arush Selvan: We had not had the NVIDIA stock crash quite yet.

Arush Selvan: And I was just playing around some experiments. Arush Selvan: I was taking a trainer from Hug and Face that had the GRP algorithm, and I was getting a really small language model, LLAMA1B, to do some reasoning and then give an answer for math questions. Arush Selvan: And I started with like a pretty simple system prompt, and I was just training the model to let it see what it did, and I had kind of manually curated some rewards in terms of what the scoring function should look like.

Arush Selvan: And I just kind of like tweeted it out, where I had an example of the model kind of looking like it's doing some self-correction and showing that the accuracy gets better as well as the length of response will initially drop once it learns to kind of follow the format.

Arush Selvan: Then it goes back up as it learns to kind of take advantage of longer chains of thought to do its reasoning. Arush Selvan: And this was not the first thing to replicate in any sense. Arush Selvan: I wouldn't really call it a true replication. Arush Selvan: It was far from the most complicated, and I think that actually caught a lot of people's imaginations, and it became kind of a thing.

Arush Selvan: So over the next two weeks after that, it just took on a life of its own where a lot of people were kind of tweeting about it and forking it and making modifications to it, Arush Selvan: And making it something you could run in a Jupyter notebook, making it more accessible, writing blog posts about it, and it was interesting.

Arush Selvan: Because it, to me, didn't feel like a thing that kind of merited this level of excitement. Arush Selvan: But what I think was catching people's imagination was that it was one file of code. Arush Selvan: It was really simple. Arush Selvan: And it invited modification in a very user-friendly, engaging way, which I like to call rubric engineering.

Arush Selvan: And so the idea of rubric engineering here is that, similar to prompt engineering, to have a model do reinforcement learning, it's going to get some reward. Arush Selvan: But what should this reward be? Arush Selvan: In the most simple version, it's just like, did it get the question right or wrong?

Arush Selvan: Like, does A equal B? Arush Selvan: But there's a lot more you can do beyond this. Arush Selvan: And so I think the single file of code exposed examples of this, where you can give the model points for things like following this XML structure. Arush Selvan: Like, if it gets a certain tag right, you give it plus one point.

Arush Selvan: If it has an integer answer that's still the wrong answer, but it's learned that the format should be an integer answer, get some points for that. Arush Selvan: And there's a lot of room here for getting creative. Arush Selvan: And for designing rules that are not just downstream evals to, for our own sake, know whether a thing is working, but to allow the model itself to know whether it's working.

Arush Selvan: And use that as feedback for going further and training more. Arush Selvan: And this is very early stages. Arush Selvan: There's a lot of things we don't know. Arush Selvan: And I think there's a lot of opportunity to get creative and explore and try things out. Arush Selvan: Such as using LLMs to design these rubrics, auto-tuning these rubrics or auto-tuning your prompts with frameworks like DSPy.

Arush Selvan: Incorporating LLM judges as part of the scoring system. Arush Selvan: And then also I think reward hacking is an issue to be very conscious of where the idea is you want to ensure that the reward model you're using is actually capturing the goal. Arush Selvan: And it doesn't have kind of these back doors where a model can kind of cheat and do something else that ultimately results in its kind of getting a super high reward without learning to do the actual task.

Arush Selvan: And following this, I have been trying to learn from those lessons of what I saw people using out in the wild and make something that is a little more robust and usable for actual projects beyond just one file of code. Arush Selvan: And this has been a kind of very recent effort.

It's not a thing that I've been telling you to go use for all your problems tomorrow. Arush Selvan: But I think it's my attempt at doing some open source research code that will help people potentially try these things out easier and answer some questions about this. Arush Selvan: And so what this really is, it's a framework for doing RL inside of multi-step environments.

Arush Selvan: So the idea here is that lots of us have built these great agent frameworks for using API models. Arush Selvan: And the hope would be that we can leverage those existing environments and frameworks to actually do RL. Arush Selvan: So here the idea is you can just create this environment thing that the model plugs into and you don't have to worry about the weights or the tokens.

Arush Selvan: You can just write an interaction protocol and then this gets fed into a trainer. Arush Selvan: And so once you build this environment, you can just kind of let it run and have a model that, once you give it some rewards, learns to get better and better over time.

Arush Selvan: And to conclude, I want to talk about what I think AI engineering might look like in the RL era. Arush Selvan: So this is all still something that is very new. Arush Selvan: We don't know whether the off-the-shelf API models are going to just work for the tasks we throw at them.

Arush Selvan: It might be the case that they do. Arush Selvan: It might be the case that they don't. Arush Selvan: One reason I think that they might not be the entire solution is that it is really hard to include a skill in a prompt. Arush Selvan: You can include knowledge in a prompt, but a lot of us, when we try something, we don't nail it the first time.

Arush Selvan: And it takes a little bit of trial and error. Arush Selvan: And it seems to be the case that models are like this as well, where a model does get better at a thing and really gets a skill nailed down by trial and error. Arush Selvan: And this has been the most promising unlock we've seen so far for these higher autonomy agents like DeepResearch.

Arush Selvan: Fine-tuning might still be important. Arush Selvan: I think a lot of people wrote off fine-tuning for a while because open models were far enough behind the frontier that a prompted frontier model API was just going to beat a smaller fine-tuned model. Arush Selvan: I think, one, we're now seeing the open and closed source gap be close enough that this is less of a concern.

Arush Selvan: A lot of people are using open source hosted models in their platforms. Arush Selvan: And also, RL, the most kind of true version of RL that DeepSeq did for their R1 model that OpenAI has talked about doing for deep research requires doing some reinforcement learning. Arush Selvan: There's a lot of challenges here.

Arush Selvan: There's a lot of research questions we don't know the answers to. Arush Selvan: But there's a lot of things that I think these skills we've learned from doing AI engineering over the past couple years translate very directly to. Arush Selvan: Which is that the challenge of building environments and rubrics is not that different from the challenge of building evals and prompts.

Arush Selvan: We still need good monitoring tools. Arush Selvan: We still need a large ecosystem of companies and platforms and products that support the kinds of agents we want to build. Arush Selvan: So I think all the stuff we've been doing is going to be essential. Arush Selvan: And it's worth looking ahead a little bit to see if we end up in a world where we have to do a little bit more reinforcement learning to unlock things like true autonomous agents or innovators or organizations that are powered by language models.

Arush Selvan: So context, prompt, diff, and we need a bunch of these examples. Arush Selvan: So how do we get these? How do we get these training examples? Arush Selvan: Kind of the first place to look is features. Features is, I mentioned a code review system that we built internally.

Arush Selvan: Maybe this one, the red to green, this is a place where the developer encountered an error, whether that's a type error or a compilation error, and then they fixed it.

So if we capture the build error at the red state, and then the diff from red to green, we can use that as training data to help the model be able to recover from mistakes. Arush Selvan: The next thing we need is a description. And the way that we did that, we just used a large language model.

So we had a large language model write a really detailed description of a change in as much words as it possibly could. Arush Selvan: And then we just kept filtering it down until it was something that was around the right level of what a human would write. Arush Selvan: So now we have this training data, and training data is kind of only half the picture of training a model.

So you have the supervised training data, and then you need to do the second part, which is the reinforcement learning. Arush Selvan: This is really where models get a lot of the power, right? We align the model's ability to what humans think is actually good code. So what is good code?

Arush Selvan: I guess, on the surface, good code is -- I mean, it's code. Arush Selvan: Good code in OCaml, because it's statically typed, is code that type checks.

Arush Selvan: We have the model perform some edit, and then we check whether or not it actually passes the test when applied to the code. Arush Selvan: So we did that.

We've done this as part of our training cycle, and we built this thing that is called CES. It's the Code Evaluation Service. Arush Selvan: You can think of it kind of like a build service, except with a slight modification to make it much faster. And that's that, first, we pre-warm a build.

Arush Selvan: It sits at a revision and is green. And then we have these workers that all day just take diffs from the model, they apply them, and then we determine whether the build status turns red or green, and then we report that error or success back up to the build function.

Arush Selvan: And through continued use of this service over the course of, like, months, we're able to better align the model to write code that actually does compile and pass tests. So the first thing is if you want to build agents and you want each agent to evolve really quickly, because when you build the first time, unless you're a magician, it's going to suck a bit.

And then it needs to improve and improve and improve and improve, right? So how do you get there? Well, let's go back to how some really good software is built. When I was a grad student, I use matrix multiplication a lot. And this is a snapshot of the generalized matrix matrix product.

And if you read the API documentation, it lays out every aspect of the input, every error code, how long it will take is also available in documentation. It's just, it just works. Right? And when you build software on top of such really well-documented, well-written software, your software also tends to be robust.

Your products tend to be robust. Even from 20 years ago when we started using machine learning to build products like, you know, there are tools like APIs that use models or pipelines of models behind them. You as a caller or a person downstream of such APIs, there is a bit of stochasticity, if I can pronounce it correct, involved, right?

You don't quite know what the result will be. And you don't quite know if it'll work for you or not. And this is despite best intentions of establishing, you know, what the input distributions are and what the output distributions are. There's always a bit of stochasticity. stochasticity. It was still okay to work with them and I'll tell you why it was okay to work with these.

But when you enter using LLMs and agents, which are really compositions of LLMs, the errors multiply a lot. And that is something that causes a lot of fragile behavior. And we'll just take a look at it. And I hope my answer is mildly surprising to you on how to avoid the fragility.

In 2009, we built a new sentiment product. It was basically to detect if a piece of news for a given company would be beneficial for that company or not. So, the input distribution, we knew which news wires, we were monitoring, we knew which language it was in. News wires also have editorial guidelines on how they write things.

So, while the API that sits in front of the model is not as clean as matrix, matrix multiply, you still have a very decent handle on what is coming into my system. And the outputs are obviously just like, you know, it's minus one to plus one, pretty much. So, like the output space is also very easy.

Training data, we built it from scratch, so we know the training data. We could have really nice, held out in time and space, test sets, and then we could establish the risk of deploying this. We could monitor it. So, despite all of this guardrail being present, we still ended up having a lot of out-of-band communication on anyone who is downstream of us.

So, for example, if you were consuming our stream of output on sentiment, we would give you a heads up. We would tell you that, hey, the model version is changing. If you have a downstream application using this as a signal, you want to test it out, things like that.

This was the landscape that's changed a lot when you think about building agentic architectures. Like, you want to make improvements to your agents every single day. You don't want to have a release cycle where there is a, you know, a purely batch regression test based release cycle. Because there are so many customers who are downstream of you who are also making independent improvements to your model.

So, I'll give you like one small example, right? So, one of the, one of the workflows that we have agents for is, for a research analyst is, I told you that structured data is something that they look at. The question here is, US CPI for the last five quarters, Q is just a quarter.

There's an agent that deeply understands the query, figures out what domain it should dispatch to, and then uses a tool. There's an NLP front end to the tool, but uses a tool to basically fetch the data, right? It turns out that the data is wrong and which is why you need the guardrails.

The data is wrong because of one character that was missed. It fetched monthly data as opposed to quarterly data. And if you're actually building a downstream workflow where you're not even exposing the table, a good research analyst would catch it. But if you're not even exposing the table, and you're just looking at an answer that says, well, it looks like the answer is 42.

It's really hard to catch these compounding errors, which is why it is easier to not count on the upstream systems to be accurate, but rather factor in that they will be fragile and they'll be evolving and just do your own safety checks. I'm talking about within my own org, people are independently operating every version of the data and analytics API tool that's coming out as better and better, but being better means being better on average.

It doesn't mean it'll be better for you as a downstream consumer. So building in some of this guardrail, I just think is good sense and that almost makes you go faster as you factor out individual agents and each agent can evolve without having these handshake signals of well, every downstream caller I have, I have to make sure that they understand what's changed.

And they sign off that I can actually release my, I can promote my new agent to like beta or production. I think we just need to like change that mindset and be more resilient. So that's one. The second thing is as much as I used to code one, one, one fine day long, long ago, I'm a manager now.

So I thought I'd talk about org structure and I don't know how many of you will resonate with it. And that software factorization is then reflected in the org structure.

If you are lucky, you have the reverse Conway law of design, but you, but you really need to rethink that as you start using different tech stacks and start building different kinds of products. Um, what do I, what do I mean? How many agents do you want to build and what should each agent do and should agents have overlapping functionality or not?

These are some basic questions. And typically it's very tempting to just say, let's just keep our current software stack and see if we can build on top of that, or let's keep our current org structure and build on top of that. And so what I've learned is on the columns here, you can see, you know, the first two columns are vertically aligned teams.

The next two columns are horizontally aligned teams. And there are some properties in the rows and what we've learned and we've actually done some reorgs. What we've learned are in the beginning, you don't really know much on what the product design is going to be. And you want to iterate fast.

It's just easier to like collapse the org. collapse the software stack and just say, here's a team go build what needs to be built and figure things out. And that's where you want like, you know, really fast iteration. You want sharing of core data models, things like that. The more you have understood this for a single product or a single agent, the more you understand what its use is and what it's good at and what it's not and what it's not.

And you actually build many, many of these agents and that's when you start thinking, okay, I can go back to the foundations of building good software and good orgs and I want to have things like optimization on it. So in our case, like guardrails are horizontal.
